<p><i><b>Take Care</b></i> is the second <a href="/wiki/Studio_album" title="Studio album" class="mw-redirect">studio album</a> by Canadian recording artist <a href="/wiki/Drake_(entertainer)" title="Drake (entertainer)" class="mw-redirect">Drake</a>, released November 15, 2011, on <a href="/wiki/Young_Money_Entertainment" title="Young Money Entertainment">Young Money Entertainment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cash_Money_Records" title="Cash Money Records">Cash Money Records</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Take_Care_November_15_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Take_Care_November_15-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tweet_115895704861421569_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweet_115895704861421569-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the follow-up to his 2010 debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Thank_Me_Later" title="Thank Me Later">Thank Me Later</a></i>. Production for the album took place during 2010 to 2011 and was handled by <a href="/wiki/Noah_%2240%22_Shebib" title="Noah &quot;40&quot; Shebib">Noah "40" Shebib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boi-1da" title="Boi-1da">Boi-1da</a>, <a href="/wiki/T-Minus_(producer)" title="T-Minus (producer)">T-Minus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Just_Blaze" title="Just Blaze">Just Blaze</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Weeknd" title="The Weeknd">The Weeknd</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jamie_xx" title="Jamie xx">Jamie xx</a>, among others. With the album, Drake sought to record a more cohesive recording than his debut album, which he felt was rushed in its development.</p>
<p>Expanding on the sonic aesthetic of his debut album, <i>Take Care</i> features an atmospheric sound that is characterized by low-key musical elements and incorporates <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electronica" title="Electronica">electronica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Post-dubstep" title="Post-dubstep">post-dubstep</a> styles. Drake's lyrics mostly eschew boastful raps for introspective lyrics that deal with topics such as failed romances, relationship with friends and family, growing wealth and fame, concerns about leading a hollow life, and despondency. The album has been noted by music writers for its minimalist R&amp;B elements, existential subject matter, conflicted lyrics, and Drake's alternately sung and rapped vocals.</p>
<p>One of the most anticipated music releases in 2011, <i>Take Care</i> experienced several delays to its release date and subsequently <a href="/wiki/Internet_leak" title="Internet leak">leaked</a> to the Internet nine days before its scheduled release.<sup id="cite_ref-Meadows-Ingram_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meadows-Ingram-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> It was promoted with eight singles— "<a href="/wiki/Marvins_Room" title="Marvins Room">Marvins Room</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Headlines_(Drake_song)" title="Headlines (Drake song)">Headlines</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Make_Me_Proud" title="Make Me Proud">Make Me Proud</a>", "<a href="/wiki/The_Motto" title="The Motto">The Motto</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Take_Care_(song)" title="Take Care (song)">Take Care</a>", "<a href="/wiki/HYFR_(Hell_Ya_Fucking_Right)" title="HYFR (Hell Ya Fucking Right)">HYFR (Hell Ya Fucking Right)</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Crew_Love" title="Crew Love">Crew Love</a>", and "Lord Knows"—all of which attained chart success, and Drake's <a href="/wiki/Club_Paradise_Tour" title="Club Paradise Tour">Club Paradise Tour</a> in 2012.</p>
<p>The album debuted at number one on the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> chart, selling 631,000 copies in its first week. By April 7, 2013, it had went double platinum selling 2,003,000 copies, according to <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_SoundScan" title="Nielsen SoundScan">Nielsen SoundScan</a>. Upon its release, <i>Take Care</i> received generally positive reviews from music critics, who commended its expansive production, emotional themes, and Drake's songwriting. It was included on year-end lists by several publications, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, both of which ranked it number one, and earned Drake a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a>.</p>
<p></p>

</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_recording">Background and recording</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background and recording">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:195px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drake_fox_theatre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Drake_fox_theatre.jpg/193px-Drake_fox_theatre.jpg" width="193" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Drake_fox_theatre.jpg/290px-Drake_fox_theatre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Drake_fox_theatre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="291" data-file-height="327" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drake_fox_theatre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.24wmf6/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Drake broadened his concert performances in between albums.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The album follows the success of Drake's 2010 debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Thank_Me_Later" title="Thank Me Later">Thank Me Later</a></i>, which became a commercial success and was well received by music critics.<sup id="cite_ref-Meadows-Ingram_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meadows-Ingram-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Diver_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> It also continued Drake's creative partnership with record producer and <a href="/wiki/Audio_engineering" title="Audio engineering" class="mw-redirect">audio engineer</a> <a href="/wiki/Noah_%2240%22_Shebib" title="Noah &quot;40&quot; Shebib">Noah "40" Shebib</a>, who had first introduced his distinct sound on Drake's breakthrough mixtape <i><a href="/wiki/So_Far_Gone_(mixtape)" title="So Far Gone (mixtape)">So Far Gone</a></i> (2009).<sup id="cite_ref-Fennessey_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fennessey-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Prior to <i>Take Care</i>, Drake also expanded his repertoire as a live performer.<sup id="cite_ref-Meadows-Ingram_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meadows-Ingram-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> For the album, he intended to have Shebib handle most of the production and record a more cohesive sound than on <i>Thank Me Later</i>, which featured disparate production duties by Shebib and others.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2010, Drake revealed the title of his next studio album would be <i>Take Care</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> In comparison to his debut album Drake revealed to Y.C Radio 1 that <i>Thank Me Later</i> was a rushed album, stating, "I didn’t get to take the time that I wanted to on that record. I rushed a lot of the songs and sonically I didn’t get to sit with the record and say, 'I should change this verse.' "Once it was done, it was done. That’s why my new album is called Take Care because I get to take my time this go-round." <sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Drake mentioned after OVO Fest 2011 that Take Care could have up to 18 songs on it, and added that <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a> contributed to the creative direction of the album and will be featured on the album as well. Drake also revealed that the album was recorded mainly in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Debating whether to submit his final cut or not, Drake's preferred release date motivated him to create a Birthday Edition, much like a deluxe edition to be released on the <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tweet_115895704861421569_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweet_115895704861421569-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some producers that were revealed to be working with Drake on <i>Take Care</i> other than <a href="/wiki/Noah_%2240%22_Shebib" title="Noah &quot;40&quot; Shebib">Noah "40" Shebib</a> (who is the main producer of the album) include <a href="/wiki/T-Minus(producer)" title="T-Minus(producer)" class="mw-redirect">T-Minus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-T-Minus_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-T-Minus-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jamie_xx" title="Jamie xx">Jamie Smith</a> from <a href="/wiki/The_xx" title="The xx">The xx</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Mtv.co.uk_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mtv.co.uk-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Boi-1da" title="Boi-1da">Boi-1da</a> (who is a long-time Drake collaborator).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> He had initially recruited <a href="/wiki/9th_Wonder" title="9th Wonder">9th Wonder</a> for the album.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> He even appeared on 9th's <a href="/wiki/Documentary" title="Documentary" class="mw-redirect">documentary</a> <i>The Wonder Year</i> and expressed his desire to make a number one hit with him.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in an interview about a month prior to the slated release date, 9th said that he was not on the album.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> 9th states that part of the reason was because he was going through an A&amp;R and playing beats for them as opposed to the artist himself, which he is opposed to.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Drake had also planned on having <a href="/wiki/Q-Tip_(rapper)" title="Q-Tip (rapper)" class="mw-redirect">Q-Tip</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DJ_Premier" title="DJ Premier">DJ Premier</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Neptunes" title="The Neptunes">The Neptunes</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> produce on the album, but those projects fell through as well.</p>
<p>Some artists that were confirmed to be collaborators with Drake on <i>Take Care</i> consist of <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kendrick_Lamar" title="Kendrick Lamar">Kendrick Lamar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chantal_Kreviazuk" title="Chantal Kreviazuk">Chantal Kreviazuk</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_3000" title="André 3000">André 3000</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rick_Ross" title="Rick Ross">Rick Ross</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lil_Wayne" title="Lil Wayne">Lil Wayne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rihanna" title="Rihanna">Rihanna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Track_listing_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Track_listing-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> He had initially reached out to <a href="/wiki/Phonte" title="Phonte">Phonte</a> of the former group <a href="/wiki/Little_Brother_(group)" title="Little Brother (group)">Little Brother</a>, who is a major influence on his career. A track was made for <i>Take Care</i> but it did not make the album due to an issue with the producer. Drake admits in an interview to "dropping the ball" on the project and is optimistic about a future collaboration with Phonte.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> He also wanted to collaborate with <a href="/wiki/Justin_Timberlake" title="Justin Timberlake">Justin Timberlake</a> stating, "The song was gonna be dope," it was produced by Noah "40" Shebib. "It was solid, a solid little look. But he's so immersed in the acting thing, and I don't blame him, he's doing great at it. He was just like, 'I really want to work. I just can't do it right now. But we'll work as soon as I'm back in the studio.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Take Care</i> expands on the low-tempo, sensuous, and dark sonic aesthetic of <i>Thank Me Later</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Primarily a <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a> album,<sup id="cite_ref-Frere-Jones_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frere-Jones-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> it has a languid, grandiose production that incorporates <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Dolan_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolan-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Amidon_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amidon-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electronica" title="Electronica">electronica</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-McCormick_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCormick-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Post-dubstep" title="Post-dubstep">post-dubstep</a> styles.<sup id="cite_ref-dubstep_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dubstep-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The music is typified by an atmospheric sound,<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> muted textures, slow <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">tempos</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> subtle <a href="/wiki/Chord_(music)" title="Chord (music)">chords</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Fleischer_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleischer-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> melodic synth tracks, <a href="/wiki/Low_fidelity" title="Low fidelity">low-end</a> <a href="/wiki/Groove_(music)" title="Groove (music)">grooves</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ritchie_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritchie-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cole_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> and sparse, ambient arrangements.<sup id="cite_ref-Diver_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Noah "40" Shebib contributed to most of the album's production with murky beats, dark synth layers, atmospheric keyboards,<sup id="cite_ref-Kot_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kot-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> moody guitar sounds,<sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> smooth piano, muffled drums,<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> dramatic flourishes,<sup id="cite_ref-Fennessey_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fennessey-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Low-pass_filter" title="Low-pass filter">low-pass filters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martens_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martens-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Although he is credited as producer for only eight of the album's 17 songs, Shebib also served as <a href="/wiki/Audio_engineering" title="Audio engineering" class="mw-redirect">audio engineer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mix_engineer" title="Mix engineer" class="mw-redirect">mix engineer</a> on the album.<sup id="cite_ref-Fennessey_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fennessey-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> His production for the album is characteristic of the Toronto hip hop scene, which experienced a mainstream breakthrough with Shebib's work with Drake, producers <a href="/wiki/Boi-1da" title="Boi-1da">Boi-1da</a> and <a href="/wiki/T-Minus_(producer)" title="T-Minus (producer)">T-Minus</a>, and singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/The_Weeknd" title="The Weeknd">The Weeknd</a>, all of whom contributed to <i>Take Care</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Evan Rytlewski of <i><a href="/wiki/The_A.V._Club" title="The A.V. Club">The A.V. Club</a></i> comments that the album is "crafted primarily around the oblique production of Drake's native Toronto—all rippling synths, distant pulses, and purposeful empty space".<sup id="cite_ref-Rytlewski_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rytlewski-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Music writers noted "late-night" and 1990s-era R&amp;B in the album's music.<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ritchie_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritchie-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> writer Frannie Kelley notes "minimalist reworkings of <a href="/wiki/TLC_(band)" title="TLC (band)" class="mw-redirect">TLC</a>'s minor-key soul and [...] trancey rhythms that land somewhere between paranoid <a href="/wiki/Sly_Stone" title="Sly Stone">Sly Stone</a> and smoked-out <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_(musician)" title="Maxwell (musician)">Maxwell</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Ryan Dombal of <a href="/wiki/Pitchfork_Media" title="Pitchfork Media">Pitchfork Media</a> comments that the music "breathes heavy somewhere between <a href="/wiki/UGK" title="UGK">UGK</a>'s deep <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quiet_storm" title="Quiet storm">quiet-storm</a> 90s R&amp;B, and <a href="/wiki/James_Blake_(musician)" title="James Blake (musician)">James Blake</a>-inspired minimalism", and interprets its subtle style to be "a direct rebuke" to the prevalence of European dance influences in mainstream music.<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> writer Todd Martens views that the album's mood and style are modelled after <a href="/wiki/Kanye_West" title="Kanye West">Kanye West</a>'s 2008 album <i><a href="/wiki/808s_%26_Heartbreak" title="808s &amp; Heartbreak">808s &amp; Heartbreak</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martens_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martens-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Other producers' tracks are more up-tempo and shift from the melancholic mood of Shebib's production.<sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Songs on the album are lengthy, sonically expansive,<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> and accompanied by playful interludes.<sup id="cite_ref-Padania_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padania-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Lauren Carter of the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Herald" title="Boston Herald">Boston Herald</a></i> writes of the song structure on <i>Take Care</i>, "Musical <a href="/wiki/Theme_(music)" title="Theme (music)">themes</a> vanish and re-appear, layers build upon layers and then strip down to bare bones as tightly wound tracks give way to gauzy, lush interludes. Most songs sound intentionally distorted and warped".<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Drake's vocals on the album feature emotional <a href="/wiki/Crooner" title="Crooner">crooning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alto" title="Alto">alto</a> vocals,<sup id="cite_ref-Martens_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martens-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Guttural" title="Guttural">guttural</a> cadence,<sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> a melodic <a href="/wiki/Flow_(music)" title="Flow (music)" class="mw-redirect">flow</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Grischow_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grischow-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> and a larger emphasis on singing than on his previous album, <i>Thank Me Later</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyrics">Lyrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lyrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width: 27%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 90%; background-color: #FFFFF0;">
<div style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div style="margin:0px 2em;">
<p>Drake's not the first to ponder such dim realities ... <i>Take Care</i>, however, raises the stakes by fully dwelling in that discomfort zone where not just sex, but every personal exchange — with admirers, among friends, within a family — starts to feel like a financial transaction. Extending the mood of his self-doubt takes Drake beyond the realm of self-pity, offering a critique of the very culture that's created him as an artist.</p>
</div>
<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">“</div>
<div style="position: absolute; right: 0px; bottom: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">”</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: center;">— <a href="/wiki/Ann_Powers" title="Ann Powers">Ann Powers</a>, on the theme of wealth, <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a><sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
<p>The album's subject matter expands on <i>Thank Me Later</i><span style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s theme of ambivalence and conflicted feelings toward fame.<sup id="cite_ref-Neyfakh_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neyfakh-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Drake's lyrics on <i>Take Care</i> address failed romances, <a href="/wiki/Missed_connection" title="Missed connection">missed connections</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> relationship with friends and family,<sup id="cite_ref-Martens_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martens-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> maintaining balance with growing wealth and fame, concerns about leading a hollow life, the passage of young adulthood,<sup id="cite_ref-Neyfakh_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neyfakh-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> and despondency.<sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kot_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kot-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The album's slower songs generally explore themes of loneliness, heartbreak, and mistrust.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The topic of women is prevalent on the album, with songs that address past and potential lovers ("Marvins Room", "The Real Her") and songs about revering ("Make Me Proud") and lavishing them ("We'll Be Fine").<sup id="cite_ref-Fleischer_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleischer-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Juan Edgardo Rodriguez of <a href="/w/index.php?title=No_Ripcord&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="No Ripcord (page does not exist)">No Ripcord</a> denotes women as "the main force in his songs - he’s consciously aware about what it takes to love them, but simply decides to thrust aside the guidelines because he's on an entirely different stratosphere from any female <a href="/wiki/Average_Joe" title="Average Joe">average joe</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The album's expositional content is interpreted by critics in relation to <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_society" title="Contemporary society">contemporary society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Newsday" title="Newsday">Newsday</a></i><span style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Glenn Gamboa views that Drake's "emotional self-doubt and realizations about [...] success", along with the album's melancholy mood, "captur[es] today's <a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">zeitgeist</a> of uncertainty and diminishing expectations."<sup id="cite_ref-Gamboa_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gamboa-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Music journalist <a href="/wiki/Ann_Powers" title="Ann Powers">Ann Powers</a> cites Drake's "predicament — the inability to locate oneself within everyday power relations" as "one that's afflicted existential antiheroes throughout modernity."<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> She denotes his point of view as that of a "biracial <a href="/wiki/Upper_middle_class" title="Upper middle class">upper middle-class</a> kid [...] from a position of privilege that few rappers would occupy", and finds his subject matter culturally significant, stating "[H]is melancholia is that of the overly sated [...] But Drake's relentless focus on the point where money empties out happiness isn't merely autobiographical. It's emblematic of our moment of crashed markets and occupied streets, and it speaks to a generation beginning to question whether the All-American, celebrity-endorsed credit card lifestyle will make them anything but bankrupt."<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Pitchfork Media's Ryan Dombal compares his "unrepentant <a href="/wiki/Omphaloskepsis" title="Omphaloskepsis">navel-gazing</a> and obsession with lost love" to <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Gaye" title="Marvin Gaye">Marvin Gaye</a>'s 1978 album <i><a href="/wiki/Here,_My_Dear" title="Here, My Dear">Here, My Dear</a></i>, adding that Drake's "penchant for poetic oversharing" makes him "an apt avatar" for the <a href="/wiki/Information_Age" title="Information Age">Information Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Drake's songwriting is characterized by wistful introspection,<sup id="cite_ref-Ritchie_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritchie-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> existential contemplation,<sup id="cite_ref-Martens_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martens-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> and minimal boasting,<sup id="cite_ref-Kot_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kot-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> with lyrics that convey frankness,<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> vulnerability, melancholia, and narcissism.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Soderberg_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soderberg-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Andy Gill of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> writes that he "eschews anger or threat for a weariness shadowed by wistful regret."<sup id="cite_ref-Gill_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gill-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Music journalist <a href="/wiki/Greg_Kot" title="Greg Kot">Greg Kot</a> comments that Drake does not "indulge in the macho poses that have dominated mainstream hip-hop for decades, and blur[s] the line between singing and rhyming", adding that he "makes his rhymes sound conversational, matter of fact, like he’s talking to the listener one-one-one".<sup id="cite_ref-Kot_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kot-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Tim Sendra of <a href="/wiki/Allmusic" title="Allmusic" class="mw-redirect">Allmusic</a> notes that his "introspective tone [...] is only rarely punctured by aggressive tracks, boasts, and/or come-ons."<sup id="cite_ref-Sendra_33-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sendra-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Drake's persona on songs shows traits of sincerity, self-doubt, regret, passive-aggressiveness,<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> and self-absorption.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kot_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kot-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Kazeem Famuyide of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Source_(magazine)" title="The Source (magazine)">The Source</a></i> explains his conflicted persona as being "arrogant enough to know his place as one of the most successful artist in hip-hop, and comfortable enough to realize his own faults in his personal life."<sup id="cite_ref-Famuyide_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Famuyide-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Jon Dolan of <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> writes that Drake "collaps[es] many moods – arrogance, sadness, tenderness and self-pity – into one vast, squish-souled emotion."<sup id="cite_ref-Dolan_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dolan-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Kevin Ritchie of <i><a href="/wiki/Now_(magazine)" title="Now (magazine)" class="mw-redirect">NOW</a></i> notes "an overwhelming sense of alienation, and sadness" on <i>Take Care</i>, calling it "an idiosyncratic, aggressively self-conscious and occasionally sentimental album".<sup id="cite_ref-Ritchie_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritchie-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release_and_promotion">Release and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Release and promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first track Drake released was "Dreams Money Can Buy" on May 20, 2011 through his October's Very Own blog. Drake mentioned this song was "A Story of Dreams, mixed with reality," and that this was not his first single off the album but that it would be included on <i>Take Care</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dreams_Money_Can_Buy_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dreams_Money_Can_Buy-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> On June 9, 2011, a second track titled "<a href="/wiki/Marvins_Room" title="Marvins Room">Marvins Room</a>" was released via his blog. Drake initially stated that the song would not be featured on <i>Take Care</i>, but because of the song's unexpected success, prompting it was released as a digital and radio single on July 22, 2011 and will be on <i>Take Care</i>. "Trust Issues" was then released shortly after on his blog, but is confirmed not to be on the album via Drake's Twitter. He explained that the song was an idea he had from <a href="/wiki/I%27m_On_One" title="I'm On One" class="mw-redirect">I'm On One</a> and made it "just for fun."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in an interview, Drake stated that Trust Issues, along with Dreams Money Can Buy, will be included in the Birthday Edition of the album.<sup id="cite_ref-Entertainment_Weekly_interview_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Entertainment_Weekly_interview-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On September 10, 2011, Drake released a new song titled "Club Paradise" on his October's Very Own blog.<sup id="cite_ref-Club_Paradise_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Club_Paradise-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> "Dropping this for our boy Avery...this was his favorite sh*t during the recording process. 2 more songs coming tonight as well. ovoxo," he wrote on his blog. On September 11, 2011, Drake released another track entitled "Free Spirit" featuring Rick Ross and blogged that another was to be released that night, as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Free_Spirit_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Free_Spirit-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that night he released a remix of Waka Flocka Flame's "<a href="/wiki/Round_of_Applause" title="Round of Applause">Round of Applause</a>". On September 23, 2011, Drake released the official album cover to <i>Take Care</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> On October 20, 2011, an unfinished version of "The Real Her" featuring only Lil Wayne was leaked online.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Real_Her_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Real_Her-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> On October 8, 2011, Drake announced on his OVO blog that <i>Take Care</i> would be pushed back until November 15 because of three sample clearances (Take Care, Cameras, &amp; Practice). It was originally to be released on his 25th birthday, October 24, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Take_Care_November_15_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Take_Care_November_15-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tweet_117752504279769088_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweet_117752504279769088-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Club_Paradise_Tour" title="Club Paradise Tour">Club Paradise Tour</a> was revealed to start in November on Twitter. However, It was revealed that the tour was delayed until after Christmas/New Year break so Drake could perform at more schools.<sup id="cite_ref-Take_Care_November_15_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Take_Care_November_15-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tweet_117752504279769088_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tweet_117752504279769088-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> A “chopped and screwed” version of the album remixed by <a href="/wiki/OG_Ron_C" title="OG Ron C">OG Ron C</a> titled <i>Chop Care</i> was released on November 29, 2011, and received over 1 million downloads in the first 48 hours. It was featured on a variety of media blogs, magazines, and newspapers. It was included on year-end lists by several publications, including The New York Times which gave major praise.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Singles">Singles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Singles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rihanna,_LOUD_Tour,_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Rihanna%2C_LOUD_Tour%2C_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg/190px-Rihanna%2C_LOUD_Tour%2C_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg" width="190" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Rihanna%2C_LOUD_Tour%2C_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg/285px-Rihanna%2C_LOUD_Tour%2C_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Rihanna%2C_LOUD_Tour%2C_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="347" data-file-height="487" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rihanna,_LOUD_Tour,_Belfast_cropped_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.24wmf6/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Recording artist <a href="/wiki/Rihanna" title="Rihanna">Rihanna</a> contributed vocals to "<a href="/wiki/Take_Care_(song)" title="Take Care (song)">Take Care</a>".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The single "<a href="/wiki/Marvins_Room" title="Marvins Room">Marvins Room</a>" impacted urban radio on June 28, 2011<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> and peaked at number twenty-one on the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Headlines_(Drake_song)" title="Headlines (Drake song)">Headlines</a>" followed as the second single from <i>Take Care</i> and was released via his blog on July 31, 2011. The song is produced by <a href="/wiki/Boi-1da" title="Boi-1da">Boi-1da</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Noah_%2240%22_Shebib" title="Noah &quot;40&quot; Shebib">40</a>, and was released to radio and iTunes on August 9, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-billboard_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-billboard-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The song debuted at number thirteen on the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>, and at number ninety-eight on the <i>Billboard</i> <a href="/wiki/Hot_R%26B/Hip-Hop_Songs" title="Hot R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Songs">Hot R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Songs</a> chart.<sup id="cite_ref-R.26B.2FHip-Hop_-_Headlines_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R.26B.2FHip-Hop_-_Headlines-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Make_Me_Proud" title="Make Me Proud">Make Me Proud</a>" features rapper <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a>, and was released via Drake's blog on October 13, 2011, as the official third single. The song was produced by <a href="/wiki/T-Minus" title="T-Minus" class="mw-redirect">T-Minus</a>, and Kromatik, and was released to iTunes on October 16, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Make_Me_Proud_iTunes_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Make_Me_Proud_iTunes-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The song has peaked at number nine on the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100.<sup id="cite_ref-Hot_100_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hot_100-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/The_Motto" title="The Motto">The Motto</a>" featuring <a href="/wiki/Lil_Wayne" title="Lil Wayne">Lil Wayne</a> impacted rhythmic radio and urban radio stations on November 29, 2011. It was re-released to rhythmic radio on January 10, 2012. It officially impacted Top 40/Mainstream radio on April 10, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Top_40_Rhythmic_Future_Releases_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top_40_Rhythmic_Future_Releases-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> The single debuted at number 18 on the <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_100" title="Billboard 100" class="mw-redirect">Billboard 100</a></i>, with first-week sales of 124,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Hot_100_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hot_100-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> It has since sold over 2 million copies in the US, becoming the most successful single from the album thus far and his third single overall to reach the milestone.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Take_Care_(song)" title="Take Care (song)">Take Care</a>" featuring <a href="/wiki/Rihanna" title="Rihanna">Rihanna</a> was released as the album's fifth single. It impacted US Top 40/Mainstream and Rhythmic radio on February 21, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Top_40_Rhythmic_Future_Releases_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Top_40_Rhythmic_Future_Releases-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Prior to its release as a single, the song entered the <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a> on November 20, 2011, at number 12. It also debuted at number 9 on the US <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100. "Take Care" became one of Drake's highest-charting songs as a solo artist in the UK and US, with first-week sales of 162,000 in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-Hot_100_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hot_100-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> In its seventeenth week on the Hot 100, the track rose to a new peak of number 7.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> As of July 2012 the single has sold over two million digital copies.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/HYFR_(Hell_Ya_Fucking_Right)" title="HYFR (Hell Ya Fucking Right)">HYFR (Hell Ya Fucking Right)</a>" was released as the sixth official single from the album. Lil Wayne is also featured on this track. A video shoot for the song took place on March 21, 2012. The video was released on April 6, 2012. It officially impacted rhythmic and urban radio on April 24, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Crew_Love" title="Crew Love">Crew Love</a>" was released as the album's seventh single in the United Kingdom on July 30, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> It peaked at number 37 on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a> and at number 80 on both the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 and the Canadian Hot 100.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> "Lord Knows" impacted radio in the United Kingdom on October 1, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_performance">Commercial performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Commercial performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The album debuted at number one on the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>, with first-week sales of 631,000 copies.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> The album also topped the Billboard <a href="/wiki/Rap_Albums" title="Rap Albums">Rap Albums</a> and <a href="/wiki/R%26B/Hip-Hop_Albums" title="R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Albums" class="mw-redirect">R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Albums</a> in its debut week.<sup id="cite_ref-Take_Care_-_Drake_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Take_Care_-_Drake-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> On January 31, 2012, it was certified <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">platinum</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a>, for shipments of one million copies in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-RIAA_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RIAA-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> By July, 2013, <i>Take Care</i> had sold 2,042,000 copies, according to <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_SoundScan" title="Nielsen SoundScan">Nielsen SoundScan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Canada, <i>Take Care</i> debuted at number one on the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Albums_Chart" title="Canadian Albums Chart">Canadian Albums Chart</a>, selling 48,000 copies in its first week.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been certified double platinum by the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Recording_Industry_Association" title="Canadian Recording Industry Association" class="mw-redirect">Canadian Recording Industry Association</a>, indicating shipments of 160,000 copies.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> In the United Kingdom, <i>Take Care</i> entered at number five on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">UK Albums Chart</a><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> and on December 16, 2011, went Gold with the British Recorded Music Industry, with 100,000 UK copies shipped to retailers.<sup id="cite_ref-BPI_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BPI-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable infobox" style="float: right; width: 24.2em; font-size: 80%; text-align: center; margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em; padding:0;" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">
<tr>
<th scope="col" colspan="2" style="font-size:120%;">Professional ratings</th>

<tr>
<th scope="col" colspan="2" style="background:#d1dbdf; font-size:120%;">Review scores</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="col">Source</th>
<th scope="col">Rating</th>

<td scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Slant_Magazine" title="Slant Magazine">Slant Magazine</a></td>

<td scope="row"><i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i></td>
<td style="vertical-align:middle">8/10<sup id="cite_ref-Soderberg_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soderberg-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><i>Take Care</i> received generally positive reviews from <a href="/wiki/Music_criticism" title="Music criticism">music critics</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, which assigns a <a href="/wiki/Standard_score" title="Standard score">normalized</a> rating out of 100 to reviews from mainstream critics, the album received an <a href="/wiki/Weighted_mean" title="Weighted mean" class="mw-redirect">average</a> score of 78, based on 34 reviews.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> John McDonnell of <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> dubbed it "an affecting masterpiece" and commended its "delicate, mellifluous sound and unashamedly candid, emotive lyrics."<sup id="cite_ref-McDonnell_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McDonnell-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pitchfork_Media" title="Pitchfork Media">Pitchfork Media</a>'s Ryan Dombal found Drake's "technical abilities" to be improved and stated, "Just as his thematic concerns have become richer, so has the music backing them up."<sup id="cite_ref-Dombal_25-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombal-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Andy Hutchins of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i> called it "a carefully crafted bundle of contradictory sentiments from a conflicted rapper who explores his own neuroses in as compelling a manner as anyone not named Kanye West."<sup id="cite_ref-Hutchins_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchins-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i> writer <a href="/wiki/Greg_Kot" title="Greg Kot">Greg Kot</a> complimented the depth of Drake's "moral psychodramas" and stated, "the best of it affirms that Drake is shaping a pop persona with staying power."<sup id="cite_ref-Kot_37-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kot-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Nitsuh Abebe of <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)">New York</a></i> wrote that the album "is full of gorgeous tones ... And the lyrics surrounding them can be rich with meaning".<sup id="cite_ref-Abebe_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abebe-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Evan Rytlewski of <i><a href="/wiki/The_A.V._Club" title="The A.V. Club">The A.V. Club</a></i> found it "plenty downbeat, but [also] gorgeous, an immersive headphone masterwork that's tender and intimate like little else in contemporary rap and R&amp;B."<sup id="cite_ref-Rytlewski_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rytlewski-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ann_Powers" title="Ann Powers">Ann Powers</a> of <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> felt that "the artfulness of this music allows me an in to that experience. I can make that leap and identify with Drake, or at least be intrigued by multiple characters in the little dramas he designs."<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_30-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Jon Caramanica of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> called it "an album of eccentric black pop that takes" hip hop and R&amp;B "as starting points, asks what they can do but haven’t been doing, then attempts those things. In the future an album like this will be commonplace; today, it’s radical." With <i>Take Care</i>, he named Drake "hip-hop's current center of gravity".<sup id="cite_ref-Caramanica_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caramanica-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In a mixed review, Kyle Anderson of <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> panned its content as "one overlong woozy monologue" and called the album a "total downer", adding that "Drake half-bakes his woozy rap-croon and glazes it with sluggish keyboard hums, ­stalling the album’s momentum".<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Alex Macpherson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> found his singing "insipid", his rapping "inert", and his lyrics "hollow", writing that "he doesn't seem to realise that introspection is only worth a damn if you're an interesting person."<sup id="cite_ref-Macpherson_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macpherson-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Globe_and_Mail" title="The Globe and Mail">The Globe and Mail</a></i><span style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Robert Everett-Green criticized Drake's lyrics as "drawling patter" and found the songs to "noodle around [...] aimlessly".<sup id="cite_ref-Green_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Accolades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, <i>Take Care</i> was the ninth best ranked album in year-end top 10 lists by music critics, based on 135 lists. It was named the best album of 2012 by the <i>Los Angeles Times</i> and <i>The New York Times</i>, and was ranked number three by <i><a href="/wiki/Now_(newspaper)" title="Now (newspaper)">Now</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>, number four by <i><a href="/wiki/Slate_(magazine)" title="Slate (magazine)">Slate</a></i>, number five by <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, number seven by <i><a href="/wiki/Fact_(United_Kingdom_magazine)" title="Fact (United Kingdom magazine)" class="mw-redirect">Fact</a></i>, number eight by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Globe_and_Mail" title="The Globe and Mail">The Globe and Mail</a></i>, NPR, and Pitchfork Media,<sup id="cite_ref-accolades_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accolades-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> number 14 by Slant Magazine,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> and number 22 by <i>Rolling Stone</i> and <i>Spin</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> It was also named as a longlisted nominee for the <a href="/wiki/2012_Polaris_Music_Prize" title="2012 Polaris Music Prize">2012 Polaris Music Prize</a> on June 14, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-longlist_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longlist-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a></i> named the album one of the classic albums of the last decade.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Take Care</i> won a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Rap_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Rap Album">Best Rap Album</a> at the <a href="/wiki/55th_Grammy_Awards" title="55th Grammy Awards" class="mw-redirect">55th Grammy Awards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> In October 2013, <a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a> named it the fourth best hip hop album of the last five years.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<td style="padding-right: 10px; text-align: right; vertical-align: top;">1.</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Take_Care_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>On the physical edition of the album, "Marvins Room" and "Buried Alive Interlude" are listed as a single track, and Kendrick Lamar is uncredited.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>"Cameras" on track 11 was co-produced by Drake, and "Good Ones Go (Interlude)" was produced by Noah "40" Shebib.</li>
<li>On the physical edition of the album, "Headlines" has a run time of 3:26 and features the "Crew Love" introduction. On the digital edition of the album, the original single version of "Headlines" is used.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt><b>Sample credits</b></dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>"Over My Dead Body" contains elements of "Sailin' Da South" as performed by <a href="/wiki/DJ_Screw" title="DJ Screw">DJ Screw</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/E.S.G." title="E.S.G.">C. Hill</a>.</li>
<li>"Shot for Me" contains a sample from "<a href="/wiki/Anything_(SWV_song)" title="Anything (SWV song)" class="mw-redirect">Anything</a>" as performed by <a href="/wiki/SWV" title="SWV">SWV</a>, written by T. Armstrong, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Alexander_Morgan" title="Brian Alexander Morgan">B. Morgan</a> and R. Smith.</li>
<li>"Take Care" contains elements of "I'll Take Care of You" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Gil_Scott-Heron" title="Gil Scott-Heron">Gil Scott-Heron</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Brook_Benton" title="Brook Benton">B. Benton</a>, remixed by <a href="/wiki/Jamie_xx" title="Jamie xx">Jamie xx</a>.</li>
<li>"Under Ground Kings" contains elements of "Neck of the Woods" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Birdman_(entertainer)" title="Birdman (entertainer)" class="mw-redirect">Birdman</a> (featuring <a href="/wiki/Lil_Wayne" title="Lil Wayne">Lil Wayne</a>), written by Batman, <a href="/wiki/Lil_Wayne" title="Lil Wayne">D. Carter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birdman_(entertainer)" title="Birdman (entertainer)" class="mw-redirect">B. Williams</a>, R. Williams and T. Jones, "<a href="/wiki/Duffle_Bag_Boy" title="Duffle Bag Boy">Duffle Bag Boy</a>" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Playaz_Circle" title="Playaz Circle">Playaz Circle</a> (featuring Lil Wayne), written by J. Banks, D. Carter, E. Conyers and T. Epps, and elements of "Farmer's Pleasure" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Jah_Cure" title="Jah Cure">Jah Cure</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Jah_Cure" title="Jah Cure">Siccature Alcock</a>.</li>
<li>"Cameras" contains excerpts from "Calling on You" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Jon_B." title="Jon B.">Jon B.</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Jon_B." title="Jon B.">J. Buck</a> and N. McCee.</li>
<li>"Doing It Wrong" contains elements of "The Wrong Thing to Do" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Don_McLean" title="Don McLean">Don McLean</a>, written by D. McLean.</li>
<li>"Look What You've Done" contains elements of "If U Scared, Say U Scared" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Playa_(band)" title="Playa (band)">Playa</a>, written by J. Peacock and <a href="/wiki/Static_Major" title="Static Major">S. Garrett</a>.</li>
<li>"HYFR (Hell Ya Fucking Right)" contains elements of "Swanging and Banging" as performed by <a href="/wiki/E.S.G." title="E.S.G.">E.S.G.</a>, written by C. Hill.</li>
<li>"Practice" contains elements of "<a href="/wiki/Back_That_Azz_Up" title="Back That Azz Up" class="mw-redirect">Back That Azz Up</a>" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_(rapper)" title="Juvenile (rapper)">Juvenile</a> (featuring Lil Wayne and <a href="/wiki/Mannie_Fresh" title="Mannie Fresh">Mannie Fresh</a>), written by D. Carter, <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_(rapper)" title="Juvenile (rapper)">T. Gray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mannie_Fresh" title="Mannie Fresh">B. Thomas</a>.</li>
<li>"The Motto" contains elements of "<a href="/wiki/She_Will" title="She Will">She Will</a>" as performed by Lil Wayne (featuring <a href="/wiki/Drake_(entertainer)" title="Drake (entertainer)" class="mw-redirect">Drake</a>), written by D. Carter, <a href="/wiki/Drake_(entertainer)" title="Drake (entertainer)" class="mw-redirect">A. Graham</a> and <a href="/wiki/T-Minus" title="T-Minus" class="mw-redirect">T. Williams</a>, and a sample of "<a href="/wiki/Baby_Got_Back" title="Baby Got Back">Baby Got Back</a>" as performed by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Mix-a-Lot" title="Sir Mix-a-Lot">Sir Mix-a-Lot</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Mix-a-Lot" title="Sir Mix-a-Lot">A. Ray</a>.</li>
</ul>
